B004LWZW8I|3.0|I am well-versed with Greek mythology, but this movie's plot and direction was all over the place. I personally think Greek mythology (and Roman, Indian, and Norse, for that matter) is ripe for action movie-making, especially with today's brilliant special effects. But after seeing this movie, I remembered things like plots, direction, casting, dialogue, and production matter.<br /><br />It certainly looks pretty, I'll give it that--and it doesn't have that slightly false, kind of strange look that \\"300\\" had to it's backgrounds, for example. Shading, shadows, and coloring is used well during battle-scenes to make certain scenes particularly enjoyable. However, the sound is off--not sure why, but at times I had the sound turned up to 75, so I could hear dialogue,which was kinda sad because it only made sense about half the time, and then dropped it to 30 during the battles.<br /><br />However, I found the film deeply boring and predictable. I looked at the clock at least 15 times during the film on DVD, and I strongly suspect I might have gotten up and left if it was in the theater. I'm not sure why it's so boring, exactly--for one, there is no reason to care for any of the characters, as they are shallowly drawn caricatures, at best, or simply faces with names, at worse--but there is also heavy use of slow-motion scenes and long, \\"zooming in\\" and \\"zooming out\\" shots of landscapes. Some scenes and transitions just took a LONG time! I strongly suspect this was a 60 minute film, extended to be become \\"production\\" length by cheesy use of slow motions, landscapes, and repeated shots of things that didn't matter (foggy walls, the metal bull, Olympus, etc). I'm frankly a little tired of the use slow-motion fight scenes in Hollywood, though I didn't even reallize it until watching this film. I'm not sure what other reviewers are talking about when they mention all the fight scenes as if there are many--I think there is 1 or 2 in the first hour of the movie--and maybe 2 or 3, including the longer one at the end, in the last hour--and other than the final fight, I think they last 2 or 3 minutes at the most. Furthermore, and most damningly, they are essentially the same scenes one sees in the advertisements. There really isn't that much else, though they are longer.<br /><br />I personally found Henry Cavill decent in a poorly-written role (for example, why would the army at the end rally to his words, since he's not part of them and has done everything possible to avoid involvement in the conflict with the army who asked him to join them?), and Frieda Pinto (as always) gorgeous and watchable, but her character was essentially a trope with little true value in moving the plot forward, and who loses her powers of predestination after she is knocked up by the hero (wow, what does THAT say?). I did not like Mickey Rourke's character or characterization, as written or performed, and I'm generally a Rourke fan...too mindlessly evil and selfish, though not particularly intimidating, maybe a little too bizarre. Why does he, when the gods ignore his call for help, decide to kill the gods by releasing the titans, rather than concluding there are no gods (which is actually more rational)? Why does he want a magic bow, the Nuclear Weapon of the Greek World, that he then uses exactly twice?  Why would he conclude the gods, who beat the titans once, wouldn't do it again?  Why does he keep switching out from amongst a series of garish masks? Why does he struggle so with speaking full sentences without an East Coast accent?<br /><br />And finally...the battle between the titans and gods at the end. Kinda good, except it made no sense according to the mythology. So the titans are a bunch of frenetic, bestial, grey-skinned, normal-sized PEOPLE with no fighting skills and who basically explode when the gods swing sticks and pointy knives into them? The gods are essentially played like superheroes in this movie, but the titans (who are supposed to be immortal, powerful GIANTS against whom the gods rebelled--and barely survived! They were imprisoned because they were too powerful to kill and too dangerous to leave loose!) basically act like the mindless, swarming vampires in \\"Blade III.\\" C'mon, seriously?<br /><br />In the end, it might be decent popcorn fare for a Saturday or Sunday afternoon when it's raining or snowing too much outide, as long as you can rent it or get it for a discounted price.

B0074JODP8|5.0|[Note: This review is of the two-disc standard DVD package, not the single-disc Blu-ray edition.]<br /><br />On November 4-5, 2011, Joe Bonamassa and his current touring band, plus some special guests, played two shows at the legendary Beacon Theatre on Broadway and 74th Street in Manhattan, New York City.  This two-disc DVD set is a chronicle of those performances, similar to Bonamassa's popular Live From the Royal Albert Hall set issued in 2009.<br /><br />It is no surprise that this DVD lives up to the high standards of its predecessor, since it featured the same brain trust (producer Kevin Shirley and director Scot McFadyen).  If anything, the more intimate Beacon allows for more closeups and camera angles than were possible at the Albert Hall.  Both the video and audio are top-shelf here, as is the packaging by Bonamassa's J&R Enterprises production company.<br /><br />Disc One begins with a clever segment featuring Joe \\"busking\\" on a New York subway platform, then making his way on foot to the Beacon.  In a wry P.S. to his liner notes in the accompanying booklet, Joe says \\"I was not able to make a single dollar busking in the subway on 72nd Street...Isn't it great to be back in New York!!!!\\"  Those lucky enough to attend one of the Beacon shows would say Joe's return was, indeed, great!<br /><br />In what was a slight reversal from his set list earlier in the year, the concert itself opens with \\"Slow Train,\\" one of Joe's originals (co-written with producer Kevin Shirley) from his recent solo LP Dust Bowl, followed by his cover of Rory Gallagher's \\"Cradle Rock\\" (with a nice bit of slide work by Joe).  An awesome one-two punch, no matter the order.  Next up is Joe's own \\"When the Fire Hits the Sea,\\" a bouncy number from his 2010 LP Black Rock featuring some tasty honky-tonk piano from keyboardist Rick Melick.  Then the mood changes considerably when Joe pays tribute to the late Gary Moore with a beautiful and evocative rendering of Moore's \\"Midnight Blues,\\" masterfully played and sung.  The tempo changes again as Joe continues with his \\"Dust Bowl\\" and fan-favorite \\"The River\\" (co-written with Bob Held, from Joe's 2004 Had To Cry Today album).  On the latter, drummer Tal Bergman and bassist Carmine Rojas lay down a rock-solid, heavy groove, and Joe breaks out the slide again to excellent effect.<br /><br />The evening's first \\"special guest,\\" vocalist Beth Hart, did a guest spot on Dust Bowl before releasing a well-received album of soul tunes in collaboration with Bonamassa (Don't Explain) in 2011, just a few weeks before the Beacon show.  Here, she tears into Brooke Benton's \\"I'll Take Care of You\\" and the Lloyd Glenn/Lowell Fulson classic \\"Sinner's Prayer\\" (with Joe's bluesy slide work complimenting Hart's singing beautifully).  If there is any justice, this DVD and Don't Explain should help Hart gain the wider audience her unique voice deserves.<br /><br />After Hart's appearance, Joe kicks the tempo back into high gear with a rollicking version of Little Walter's \\"You Better Watch Yourself,\\" also from Dust Bowl.  This is followed by a couple of tunes from Black Rock, a cover of Bobby Parker's \\"Steal Your Heart Away\\" and a touching rendering of Leonard Cohen's \\"Bird On a Wire\\" that practically re-invents the song (with the help of Joe's Gigliotti Telecaster-style guitar).<br /><br />Speaking of Cohen, an American musical treasure, the evening's second \\"special guest\\" is another of America's great singer-songwriters, the amazing John Hiatt. One of the highlights of the DVD is his collaboration with Joe on the haunting \\"Down Around My Place.\\"  Unforgettable.  Their blues-rock rendition of Hiatt's \\"I Know a Place\\" (which Joe covered on Black Rock) is also a treat.<br /><br />Following Hiatt's appearance, Joe and the band tear into Joe's original \\"Blue and Evil,\\" my favorite song on Black Rock.  Glad to see this one back on the set list, Joe!<br /><br />The evening's final \\"special guest\\" had to have been special indeed forBonamassa, since Paul Rodgers was lead singer for Free, one of Joe's favorite British blues-rock influences.  Rodgers' distinctive voice is in fine form on two Free classics, \\"Walk in My Shadow\\" and \\"Fire and Water\\" (Joe covered the former on his 2008 album Live From Nowhere in Particular).  Quite a treat watching Joe channel Paul Kossoff while sharing the stage with his hero's former bandmate.<br /><br />Disc One concludes with the set-closer \\"Mountain Time,\\" another fan favorite for nearly a decade (it debuted on Joe's album So It's Like That in 2002), with an intro featuring a medley of the Bonamassa instrumentals \\"India\\" and \\"Django.\\"  And then there's the encore, a blistering version of Mose Allison's \\"Young Man Blues\\" that gives The Who's rendition a run for its money.  Rojas and Bergman in particular have a blast on this one, and Joe makes splendid use of both necks on his \\"Hotel California\\" Gibson ES-1275.<br /><br />Disc Two features two Bonus songs, a cover of Warren Haynes's plaintive \\"If Heartaches Were Nickels\\" and Joe's crowd-pleasing acoustic tour de force \\"Woke Up Dreaming\\" (co-written with Will Jennings, from Joe's 2003 album Blues Deluxe).  Also included is \\"A Friend Stops By,\\" an amusing backstage visit by David Crosby (who shows Joe a couple of tuning tricks), and \\"A Busker's Tale,\\" a tongue-in-cheek mini-documentary about Joe's busking adventure on the 72nd Street subway platform. Finally, there is a wonderful picture gallery (set to the studio version of \\"You Better Watch Yourself\\" and Joe's \\"Black Lung Hearache,\\" both from Dust Bowl), including shots of some other \\"special guests\\" (and guitars!) that didn't make it onto the DVD. Nice.<br /><br />The package is not without some relatively minor flaws:  Rodgers' name is misspelled, for instance, and the list of guitars used omits the Ernie Ball Music Man Steve Morse model Joe uses on \\"Mountain Time.\\"  More significantly, the booklet has \\"Fire and Water\\" and \\"Walk in My Shadow\\" reversed from theorder they appear on the DVD.  Oops!<br /><br />This well-produced package will prove a treat to long-time fans, and newcomers encountering Bonamassa's virtuosity and showmanship for the first time will find it hard to resist as well. It isn't easy these days to find a band focused more on the music than on the stage act, but Bonamassa, Melick, Rojas, and Bergman put out tight, excellent music with honesty and joy.  They're clearly enjoying themselves, and you will too. Highly recommended!

014241493X|5.0|I have written a five-star review of this book on my \\"noteworthy YA fiction\\" blog: The Stuff That Isn't Twilight, but I'll post an excerpt of it below:<br /><br />Let me get this out of the way now: John Green is a wonderfully talented author.  I whizzed through \\"Paper Towns\\" being so empathetic to Q and thoroughly intrigued by Margo.  The whole idea of who we see when we look at people, and who they are behind the mask is portrayed beautifully by Q's inability to let go of his childhood image of Margo and Margo's desperation for people to both see and not see her.  After finishing, I questioned my own way of assessing people through what they've said to me and their actions, and I wondered if any of that was real.  Who are the people around us? Who are they to their parents, their friends, their lovers? Are they the same person to everyone? If not, is that okay? Are we all liars?  These are the kinds of questions Green brings up throughout \\"Paper Towns.\\"  But Green also looks at the selfishness of identity, and the struggle to satisfy our own sense of self. Is what we want ourselves to be more important than what others need us to be? Is there a middle ground? Green creates Q and Radar and Margo each with similar and different wants and needs and allows them to all intertwine and crash.  Readers of any gender (as far as I can tell, since Q is a boy and I am a girl) will be able to understand and sympathize with Q's frustration and obsession and desperation for finding Margo and discovering how important it is to find out who she actually is.<br /><br />\\"Paper Towns\\" is the perfect novel for ages fourteen and up. Both the beginning trip between Q and Margo and the road trip with Q and Radar are incredibly engaging and have the perfect amount of hilarity, seriousness, and teenage crises. You won't be able to stop thinking about the book until you finish it.  All the questions running through Q's brain will run through yours, too, and you won't be able to help lookingat your own world differently.  Green wrote a simply inspiring piece that every teenager should pick up.

B005OZE9SA|5.0|I'm using this unit to set up a Linux Digital Audio Workstation. It works out of the box with a recent ubuntu install or linux mint if you prefer. You will probably want a realtime kernel and use jackd to take full advantage of this device. Without using a jackd interface or a realtime kernel audacity records 96kHz but skips when duplexed, I mark this up to not having sophisticated controls or realtime enabled.<br /><br />The following settings are the best I've been able to manage in jackd2 without and xruns on a realtime kernel(using qjackctl) (I'm also no expert)<br /><br />Frames / Period: 128<br />Sample Rate: 96000<br />Periods / Buffer: 3<br /><br />Which gives a predicted latency of 4ms<br />With a 10ft 1/4 inch patch cord (to run jack_iodelay) I got a consistent 8.24 ms overall latency (not bad imho).<br /><br />Without the realtime kernel you should expect xruns with these settings, my computer is a sandy bridge pentium 2.2ghz with 6gb ram and a ssd.<br /><br />We have used this with an AT2020, AT2021. SM58 microphones and the sound quality is quite high. The preamps deliver what seems like no noise under usual conditions. Simple two mic recordings of band practices sound outstanding. I've also tried some higher gain recordings of weird ambient sounds (rain crickets) and still received very little noise from the device itself but can hear everything that happens in the house.<br /><br />Update:<br />I've found the above setting in jack don't work well for processing live guitar effects in rakarrack. Here are the best setting I could come up with<br /><br />Frames/Period: 512<br />Sample Rate: 48000<br />Peiods/Buffer: 3<br />Realtime<br />Soft Mode<br /><br />Qjackctl lists the latency of 32ms but I have not tried measuring i with jack_iodelay. All the included effects of rakarrack work on my computer for these while booted in the (hard) realtime kernel

0439023521|5.0|In the ruins of America lies Panem a country of wealthy snobs keeping genuine people under a strict bootheel. As punishment for an almost centuries old rebellion each year The Hunger Games are held. Two children (a boy and girl between the ages of 12 to 18) are chosen from each of the 12 Districts of Panem. The Games are anything but fun, but rather a sadistic display of violence and murder in the tradition of Gladiator combat. When her sister is chosen, 16 year old Katniss Everdeen sacrifices herself instead taking the childs place.<br /><br />Does the young Bow Hunter have what it takes to survive?<br /><br />I first became aware of \\"The Hunger Games\\" because of the forthcoming film. I didn't really pay much attention to it. Then my nephew told me of the book, he's 16 and not much of a reader so if he liked it it occurred to me to check it out. I ordered it here and got it two days later on a Monday. By Wednesday I was finished. I highly recommend this book, but be warned it's brutal, the author Suzanne Collins has a way of putting the reader right in the thick of things. I could feel myself in the arena with Katniss, feel the blistering heat of the wall of flame, feel the sting of the Tracker Jackers and shiver along with Peeta as he succumbed to blood poisoning.<br /><br />\\"The Hunger Games\\" is a must read for Genre and Non-Genre fans alike.

0439023513|2.0|I started reading The Hunger Games trilogy Monday, after I decided that I was probably missing something since it's such a phenomenon (then again, I also thought this of The Twilight Saga and..well...anyway.). The Hunger Games was amazing. Shattering. Literally had me in tears multiple times and I immediately bought tickets to the movie in IMAX. Catching Fire was a little...well...muddled, but second books in trilogies usually are and it wasn't by any means BAD, just...completely different from what I thought it would be. I wondered how the hell it was going to end, and eagerly moved into Mockingjay. After I finished, one word came to mind:<br /><br />Seriously?<br /><br />She completely ignores personal relationships, both past and present, when they were initially the basis of the Katniss character and why she did what she did the way she did. She launches from one scene to another with dizzying speed and no real segue to speak of. Characters she had thus spent the entire trilogy making you care for were killed off with no more than a seemingly distracted sentence; several times I had to re-read a paragraph to make sure that character had really just been killed.<br /><br />There's absolutely no sense of the characters as people anymore. She'd spent so much time in character development, in little scenes that somehow gave startling insight into who the other characters were, that I found her blithe insertion of names to be trite and almost insulting in their lack of introduction. Basically, for the most part, you can tell who died because she essentially tosses in random names with no personalization whatsoever. Token body count fluffer to your left, random_dude_01 to your right. And while I understand that with war comes senseless violence, the \\"final battle\\" scene is exceedingly gratuitous, even to someone as bloodthirsty as I consider myself.<br /><br />From someone who had spent so much time making the scene of Rue's death so heartbreakingly poignant to go from that to this pile of crap...maybe she took a whack to the head we don't know about while writing this? I don't know.<br /><br />Then there's the story. It's so unbelievably rushed, crammed, and at some points nonsensical that I was at times confused. It almost seems like she was thinking about making into a four-book series, said screw it, and crammed everything in just to finish it off.<br /><br />I gave it two stars because...hm. I actually don't know. I guess I gave it two stars because the first book got to me so completely, and the second one seemed like it could've been going somewhere great. Two stars for sentimentality, but on its own? Maybe half a star. I truly can't remember the last time I was so disappointed in a book.

B005974CNA|5.0|I got this CD when it first came out. Dream Theater is probably my favorite band, definitely were years ago. Systematic Chaos and Black Clouds & Silver Linings, the two prior Dream Theater albums, were very fun for the first two days, and dropped deeply in quality as time passed by. I didn't like the direction that Mike Portnoy was pushing DT in. I was very excited when I found out MP left DT, and was replaced by Mike Mangini.<br /><br />This album is definitely a DT style album; MP leaving didn't really change that aspect, but changed some of the modern elements MP seemed to try and integrate into the band. The drumming is great, if not mixed too low..though I am not too put off by the low mix of the drums. Luckily, the bass is mixed higher than previous albums, and the album feels fuller.<br /><br />This album is amazing. It starts off strongly with the slow intro and literally epic keyboard riff with \\"On the Backs of Angels,\\" a very progressive song that isn't immediately catchy.<br /><br />The next two tracks, \\"Build Me Up, Break Me Down\\" and \\"Lost Not Forgotten,\\" would be the 2 tracks I would leave as B-sides. BMUBMD is alright for a few listens but I mostly skip it. LNF is much better but seems to lack direction sometimes. I am finding it growing on me, so I may eventually retract my statement of it being a B-side.<br /><br />The first ballad, \\"This is the Life,\\" gets a lighter, very atmospheric mood going, and it feels wonderful. Labrie shines here, as do Petrucci and Rudess.<br /><br />Next up are two \\"mini-epics\\" or longer songs, \\"Bridges in the Sky\\" and \\"Outcry.\\" BITS has, without a doubt, one of the best choruses ever, certainly DT's best chorus in my opinion. It's just an over all well-constructed song, and may be my favorite. Outcry is a very dense and technically proficient song, as if they took much inspiration from \\"The Dance of Eternity\\" and gave it lyrics. Powerful duo of songs.<br /><br />Another ballad comes in, \\"Far From Heaven,\\" and it's a really simple, short song, and it is really beautiful, if not very melancholy. It also leads into the album's stellar gift, \\"Breaking All Illusions.\\" (There is a musical connection between these two songs, though I forget what it is exactly). BAI is one of those songs that just flies by because you don't want it to stop. It's not as progressive as Outcry, but it's progressive nonetheless. So many nice things...and it's prog in every sense of the genre's meaning. A true DT song, I think many would say.<br /><br />The album could have ended there, but DT gives us a closure in the form of \\"Beneath the Surface.\\" This is just a gorgeous song. Pretty simple (no bass and very minimal percussion), with acoustic guitar plucking and various keyboard arrangements that just fill out the song perfectly. There is a keyboard solo that would theoretically be out of place, but it actually makes the song even better.<br /><br />All in all, I gave this 5 stars because it is a great return to form after the last 2 albums, and almost all of the songs are at least 8/10. If you fell out of love with DT, this album may pull you back in, if you're not over the prog metal genre (of which I'm mostly not a fan).

I am a sucker for a good romantic trilogy.  So, without pre-reading anything about the books or the author, I began to read this at 8pm last night knowing I had to get up early in the morning.  I read it straight through until 3am this morning and finished the book.  I am still unsure as to why I kept reading it.  I was completely annoyed with the obviously British phrasing, even though all the characters are American born and raised. I was shocked when it made reference to the year being 2011.  To me, the most realistic dialogue in the whole book came from the emails they sent back and forth to each other.  I found those to be entertaining and actually made me smile.  So here comes the \\"mixed\\" part of my review......with all of the things that drove me nuts about the horrible writing, I was hooked and could not put the book down.  I was actually drawn into the characters despite wanting their conversations to sound like actual people of their age and setting.  However, I am not sure I was drawn in enough to spend $10 to read the next book and relive the same scenes over and over again.  I gave this book 2 stars because of the author's inability to make me feel like this is a book about 2 young Americans in the 21st Century.  It would have found it much more believable if the setting were in England.

0307593312|4.0|1Q is in some ways vintage Murakami. I prefer his fictions way more than any of his non-fiction works. There is no middle of the road with him - Murakami's works are either strictly fact based or wildly fantastic and this was the latter. Part I is what I imagine how a mental patient slowly losing grip with reality must feel. Part II unfolds a love story. Part III gives a satisfying Holywood ending to a tale of destinies.<br /><br />Reading Murakami, I usually suspend my disbelief and allow myself to be led by the author to different times and places as fantastic as a Tim Burton movie. There is just enough groundedness in the characters to make me identify with them. Throughout the journey, I allow myself to get into discussions with the author about the meaning of cults, the need for spiritual life, ghosts, the meaning of sex, love and alas, life. How many people have lived most of their lives, matas without dohtas or dohtas without matas? One message of the story seems to be: whatever happens, if you find a part of you missing, you should do everything you can to try to reunite with that missing part.<br /><br />I don't have time to read a lot of fiction and it took me a while to finish this book. There were times I felt that Murakami might have gotten just a little too sure of himself and liked the sound of his own voice just a little too much. But still I felt sad and a little lost when the book was finished.<br /><br />And that isn't a bad ride.

076790592X|5.0|This is a very short (192 pages), but engaging narrative about two people: the author Mitch Albom, and his terminally ill mentor, Professor Morrie Schwartz.  About sixteen years after his graduation from Brandeis University, Albom was moved to contact Schwartz when he saw a television program that detailed how Schwartz was dealing with amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (Lou Gehrig's Disease).  Mitch promises to visit Morrie on Tuesdays (the day on which he previously attended Morrie's classes).  The book summarizes fourteen visits, which Albom treats as a graduate course on life itself.  During these visits, they discuss the world in general, self-pity, regrets, death, family, emotions, aging, money, love, marriage, culture, forgiveness, perfection and good-bye.  The student is plainly impressed by the manner in which the professor faces his fate.<br /><br />Morrie's outlook is not new; and the book does not explain the bases for his philosophy of life.  Suffice it to say that the worldly successful student is awed by the outlook of his old professor, and by Morrie's ability to deal with personal tragedy.  It brought back memories of Bob Korek, a neighborhood high school football star who died from ALS, and memories of my father confronting his final illness.<br /><br />The book is well written, albeit not in perfect conformity with the rules of grammar.  The author earns his living in the public news media.  He knows the style that attracts the public; and he writes in that style.  The reader is never in doubt as to the meaning of the author's words.  You may question his motives, but not his meaning.<br /><br />It is a very quick read.  I read it on a Tuesday.
B000E6EGXM|5.0|Britten was clearly a major artist, and when he kept it simple he often soared to great heights.  I listened again to his delightful \\"Simple Symphony\\" and wondered what wonders he might have created had he lived before Schoenberg made tonality unfashionable.  I must admit that sometimes I find his bracing, astringent, haunted, joyless vitality a wet blanket.  Is it too reactionary to suggest that among mid-century British choral composers, Gerald Finzi wrote far more loveable (and equally profound) work?  Of course, I realize that Britten created an original, if austere sonic landscape-- perhaps the closest analogy in theatre would be Samuel Beckett.  But then again, we don't wait for Godot in the home theatre every night.<br /><br />Of all the great composers, Britten is probably the one who would have benefited the most from having his kettle drums confiscated.  And Nietzsche's comment about Wagner's music being sung only by ruined voices too frequently applies in this case as well.  Perhaps I'm only reacting to certain late recordings by Peter Pears (I do recognize that Britten had an extraordinary gift for setting poetry to music).<br /><br />Bitterness, rendered into sound, is not a gift to art.  Sometimes Britten doesn't realize he's crossed a line meant to separate personal suffering from public creation.<br /><br />All this just to excuse myself for admitting that-- after owning this recording for years-- and admiring it,  I have yet to find access to it. 1400069289|4.0|\\"The Power of Habit\\" is a reasonably enjoyable introduction to basic habit foundations. The author's working definition of habits involves a three-part Loop, with a Cue (the action's initiator), a habitual Routine (which may be physical or emotional), and a Reward (which \\"informs\\" your mind about a particular Loop's value).  The Loop can be changed over time, is mostly subconscious, blends past experiences and present events, interacts with other mental functions, contains social expectations, often gives a powerful sense of anticipation and craving, and can become stronger and more automatic over time (but even a single Loop can establish a solid habit).<br /><br />Perhaps the book's strongest points are the powerful and often charming stories and anecdotes used to illustrate the author's points (although he seems to generalize a bit too much from them). After describing the stories, the author discusses psychological and neurological information, and then illustrates how we can use this information to modify our habits.  Those wanting to change habits are most successful when using the conscious mind to find better Cues and Rewards, instead of beating ourselves up when we fail.  The author generally uses case studies to make points. Case studies, although appealing and often quite accurate, are among the weaker types of evidence and require your own evaluative judgment, so you need to consider the author's credibility and experience. He has been an investigative reporter for the NY Times and author of several articles apparently discussing abuses of corporate power, so use your judgment in evaluating his scientific credentials.  The book's bibliographic sources are a blend of primary-source journal articles, secondary-source books, and media articles.<br /><br />This is a complex topic, and the book seems most useful for the general reader who may not have had these concepts introduced through a formal psychology course or courses. I found it quite easy to understand and follow, although some analogies seem off the point and I noticed several plays on the emotions. Although I am not sure this will be a classic, and you shouldn't expect anything really profound, it is written interestingly enough to sell well.and and follow, although some analogies seem off the point and I noticed several plays on the emotions. Although I am not sure this will be a classic, and you shouldn't expect anything really profound, it is written interestingly enough to sell well.

0670022950|5.0|I have to admit that some of the assertions in Pinker's latest book made me uncomfortable at first (the concept of the 'civilizing process,' the idea that mass incarceration in the United States really has contributed to the decline in crime, to name a couple), but Pinker backs-up everything so thoroughly that I found myself utterly convinced by the end. This is the first book of his I've read, but I was amazed at how well Pinker translates extremely complex and difficult concepts for ordinary readers. Pinker brings incredible clarity to different topics without ever being reductionist.<br /><br />I feel like there's been a trend of contrarian (or revisionist, if you like) histories over the past few years, but what sets Pinker's book apart is that it does not defy conventional wisdom for the sake of standing out from the crowd. He's done his homework, and it shows.<br /><br />To offer some criticisms: I think Pinker refers back to evolutionary psychology to explain highly specialized psychological phenomena a little more than necessary, and in his comparison of catastrophic events from past time periods, I think he ends up using estimates for the death tolls (particularly for genocides of the past) that are a bit high. These criticisms are extremely minor, and I didn't think they warranted taking off a star.

His points are, at times, simply expressed opinion or based on a \\"rough study\\" done some time back. Finally and significantly, the lack of humanity apparent in his thesis is chilling.<br /><br />While \\"Capitalism and Freedom\\" is, by its significant  position in the popular political/economic literature of the last several decades, an important book, it is concerning in both its thesis and methodology.<br /><br />To borrow from Fran Leibowitz, \\"In the Soviet Union, capitalism triumphed over communism. In this country, capitalism triumphed over democracy.\\" Friedman's role in this tragic triumph was not insignificant.

B005LAIGFU|3.0|Tilda Swinton is stripped of her exotic androgyny as the guilt-ridden mother,Eva, who gloried in her formerly independent state of freewheeling travel and love of Franklin her husband.The book and film dealt with a taboo: what if a mother doesn't like her son or can't bond with him?Debate centred upon maternal ambivalence and bad parenting.What if a child felt unwanted, would his lack of love be turned into a weapon of manipulative psychopathy?Pregnancy doesn't kickstart the milk of human kindness.The parents are dishonest in their dealings with Kevin's bad behaviour,lack of empathy and subtle malevolence.Eva puts up with his negative behavoiur and never really confronts him,even though she knows she should.Franklin(JC Reilly) buries his head in the sand,pretends everything is hunky-dory,overcompensating for Eva's lack of affection for Kevin. Kevin levers himself between the two of them and exposes their weakness.Kevin is no Damien:he's both arrogant and cruel to mother and sister,disrespects but loves his dad,but he's also vulnerable, though he's the context of vast cunning and manipulative evil.Kevin has picked up from a very young age he is the cause of his mother's loss of freedom,so he is perfectly capable of talking but she thinks he intentionally holds back on his language development out of spite as he doesn't want to please her. Similarly, she thinks he is perfectly capable of bowel control and toilet training, but until the age of 6 he is still in nappies, making her life intentionally as awkward as possible.<br /><br />Of course due to the problems of running time and the slashing of the budget,Ramsay has had to concertina the story in a flashback structure(we leave out the epistolary nature of the novel)stripping down the literary conceit,giving us a delayed reveal through net curtains veiling the worst of Kevin's misdemeanours. We get the splintering of time,the symbolism of imagery with the colour red, tomatoes,paint,soup cans,to immerse you in the tormented, fragmented memories of the woman who is depicted as the victim,nailed teeth-grindingly and reluctantly to motherhood,and the monstrous nature of her evolving son. Jasper Newell and Ezra Miller are brilliant younger and older Kevins.This is not treated as an issue film,we are given the subjective view of Eva looking back and asking what went wrong from Kevin's conception to his murders,in the story of her family. Eva instantly feels a connection with her daughter in a way she never did for Kevin. Whether that makes him jealous or whatever, he always uses her to torment Eva further. Once her daughter loses her eye (because of Kevin or not, who knows) then Kevin constantly picks on her about it. Swinton plays her part superbly well,one of anxiety,coldness and tension. The cinematography and sound design are beautiful as is the editing, just excellent. However where the film falls down is perhaps in the plot and execution of story. We're left almost wondering what the entire purpose of the film was,it's an impressionistic rendition of a mother's experience and the nature of her unconditional love for a confused, complex,damaged and disturbed child.Brave but ultimately flawed.

0385528752|5.0|Heard and enjoyed SWITCH: HOW TO CHANGE THINGS WHEN CHANGE IS HARD (Random House Audio), written by Chip Heath & Dan Heath and narrated by Charles Kahlenberg.<br /><br />The authors answer the question: Why is it so hard to make lasting changes in our communities and in our lives? The primary obstacle is a conflict that's built into our brains, which are controlled by two different systems--the rational mind and the emotional mind. But if we can overcome this tension, then change can come quickly.<br /><br />For example, they start off the book with a fascinating study about moviegoers who are given stale popcorn. it seems that people eat more popcorn when you give them a bigger bucket. Period. So if you want them to eat less popcorn, just give them smaller buckets. You don't have to spend all sorts of time trying to influence their motivation or understanding.<br /><br />Then they discuss an alarm clock called the Clock that has wheels. You set it at night and when it goes off in the morning, it rolls off the nightstand and forces you to chase it down. Our rational side in this instances wants to get up at 5:45 a.m., but our emotional side wants nothing more but to stay inside a warm cocoon of sheets to get a few more minutes of sleep.<br /><br />The book presents many other examples, taken from a wide variety of situations. The bottom line is presented through the analogy of an Elephant and his Rider. The Rider is your logical brain. The Elephant is your heart. If you want the elephant to move, you need to engage both the Rider and the Elephant. Or if you want to change behavior, you must direct the Rider (by providing clear direction), motivate the elephant (by engaging emotions) and shape the Path (by creating the best environment).<br /><br />SWITCH is a fine book to read for any individual or organization going through change--or contemplating that possibility.

B005HV6Y5W|5.0|\\"Where were you when I laid the foundations of the Earth? When the morning stars sang together, and all the sons of God shouted for joy?\\"<br /><br />This verse of Scripture from the book of Job opens Terrence Malick's latest masterpiece, entitled The Tree of Life. After these words appear over a black screen and fade out into the darkness, a mysterious flame illuminates the center of the screen, followed by a distant voice which breaks the silence - \\"Brother. Mother. It was they who led me to Your door.\\"<br /><br />Thus begins the most beautiful and complex film that you are likely to see this decade. It is not a film in the traditional sense of the word. This is not a plot-driven film. What we have here is the journey of life in words and images - a prayer to God which questions His motives, which longs to understand Him, which calls upon Him, and yearns to be near Him at all times.<br /><br />The film follows Jack O'Brien (Sean Penn) on the anniversary of his younger brother's death. Jack, a skilled architect, is disillusioned with the world around him. \\"The world has gone to the dogs\\", Jack ponders, as he stares blankly out of the window of a high-rise building. Jack leaves his office and prays to God - \\"When did you first touch my heart?\\" Almost immediately, we are whisked back and forth, through past, present, and beyond, to the creation of the galaxies and the Earth, to the brief reign of the dinosaurs, and the beginning of human existence, until, finally, we reach \\"the end of time\\".<br /><br />Throughout the film, we revisit Jack's tormented childhood in rural Texas in the 1950s. We are introduced to his family, led by his parents (Brad Pitt and Jessica Chastain). His angelic mother teaches Jack and his two brothers the ways of grace (love, forgiveness), while his father represents the way of nature (selfishness, greed). These conflicting worldviews serve as a catalyst for Jack's spiritual and existential struggle, which drives the second act of the film.<br/><br />Mind you, this synopsis does the film no justice. This film cannot be defined in simplistic terms, nor can it be categorized into any specific genre of film. This is a film that defies convention. It redefines the way that we experience cinema. It is alive, full of wonder and awe. It is poetry. As a friend of mine said after the first screening, this film is a force. It will be a different experience for each individual. You will get out of this film exactly what you bring to it. For me, it is quite possibly the most spiritually significant experience that I have ever had in a theatre. It allowed me to see God portrayed in a way that is respectful, unique and all-encompassing. The grace and mercy of God saturates every frame of this film. Every element - from the beautiful and haunting imagery, to the ethereal soundtrack, to the understated performances, to the philosophical musings on existence - works together in perfect harmony to create a work of art that you will never forget. The performances are uniformly excellent, and the direction from Terrence Malick is as perfect as ever. Not since Bergman has an artist tackled these complicated themes with such respect and originality.<br /><br />This is the best film that I have ever seen.<br /><br />Rated PG-13 for thematic elements. 139 minutes. ***** out of ***** Fox Searchlight Pictures. Directed by Terrence Malick.

B0029LHWFO|3.0|To start, I enthusiastically voted for Mr. Obama in 2008 believing that we finally had a candidate who understood the middle-lower classes (since it appeared he was one of us) and would give voice to our needs in government.  After nearly four years of watching Mr. Obama grow our government while reducing our liberties, reneging on most (if not all) his promises and general lackadaisical (if not absent) leadership, I am one of the many Obama supporters who wants to fill in the many blanks I never truly realized I had about our president.  I want to have a better understanding of why the man I see on my television does not match up to the politician I expect him to be (keeping in mind that I do have a realistic, if not pessimistic, view of politicians to begin with).<br /><br />I decided to start with finally reading \\"Dreams from My Father,\\" considering that it is Mr. Obama's autobiography and a book I have been meaning to read.  I went into it with not only an open mind, but a hope that I would see the man who so deeply appealed to me in 2007-2008.  Unfortunately, I must say that if I read this book back then I would have changed my favorable opinions toward him.<br /><br />While there is no denying that Mr. Obama is an articulate, thoughtful writer, in all his espousing on race relations he often missed the forest for trees.  We all can (and should) acknowledge that institutions such as slavery and Jim Crow are disgraceful blemishes on the face of our nation.  But why would Mr. Obama, as intelligent as he is, subscribe to racial generalizations?  I would expect Mr. Obama to be a stronger man than this.  If we are to discuss white supremacy as some pervading, all powerful establishment, then why are we not to acknowledge that there are many white families who struggle, who can be found in \\"ghettos\\" and on public assistance?  It does such a disservice to all of us, but especially the black community, to scapegoat problems such as Mr. Obama does in this book without a counter examination of self-sufficiency and responsibility.  It is particularly troubling to still scapegoat after all the hard work accomplished during the civil rights years.  I am not a naive person, but I do want to believe that my friends and I do not view each other as natural enemies - that a common ground can be reached through a universal humanitarian respect.  According to Mr. Obama's worldview in this book, such a common ground can never be reached because white racism is so deeply entrenched as to never be eradicated.  It reminds me of my former studies in radical feminism, where the patriarchy is so pervading that I as a woman will be degraded at every turn.  To me, this worldview is stifling, counter-intuitive and a waste of time.<br /><br />Further, I was surprised to see very little reflection by Mr. Obama which would explain why he would so easily subscribe to a more radical (and divisive) view of what it means to be black.  Here is a bi-racial child who was absolutely loved (particularly by his white grandparents), a child who was not the recipient of abject racism, who went on to accomplish many great things in his young life and was given opportunities that most white kids, let alone black kids, would never have.  Yet, when he receives his acceptance to Harvard Law School (to use one instance from the book), it is met with ambivalence and, dare I say, regret.  Most young people, regardless of situation, would take more than a moment to realize what an extraordinary accomplishment that is and would literally visualize the doors opening for them.  Not Mr. Obama.  Instead, he feels a betrayal to his blackness to put it simply.  Pretty disappointing.<br /><br />While I never like to speculate on what people feel, I suspect that Mr. Obama gravitated towards his radical identity because of the deep hole left by his father's absenteeism.  I submit that perhaps Mr. Obama felt closer to his black identity, and was a bit more militant about it, not simplybecause of how he \\"looked\\" to the outside world but because he was desperate to understand and feel a part of his African family, since his white family was there and accessible to him.  Looking at it from that perspective, his racial ideas are a bit easier to swallow and I am sympathetic. If Mr. Obama chose to focus his book a bit more on his personal search for racial identity as a product of a bi-racial, broken marriage, instead of the many musings on the radical ideas he was deliberately exposing himself to, I would have enjoyed his perspective more.  In fact, the times during the book when he does get personal, such as his internal discussions about his family, are revelatory and interesting.  That said, I am just speculating because Mr. Obama never makes these connections himself.<br /><br />Is Mr. Obama the same man who wrote this book in 1994? 1250006341|4.0|Complimented by the photography of Nick Briggs and the revealing Foreword by Julian Fellowes, this sumptuous volume belongs on any coffee table--furniture which the Edwardians apparently did not need. What a marvelous companion to the Carnival Films/Masterpiece Theate two-season co-production. When I first saw the book advertised I assumed it was the novel which inspired the series on PBS, but come to learn that it was the series which inspired the book! Not a plotted novel in fact; rather, a wonderful easy-to-digest description of the life of British gentry life just before, during and after the Great War. The start of the modern era with electricity, motorcars and telephones--much to the disgust of the dowager Countess, Violet, who deplores any change as a threat to the Social Establishment, where people knew their place both Upstairs and Downstairs.<br /><br /> Heavy to hold but filled with sumptuous scenes of gorgeous (and humble) interiors, with exquisite gowns and minutely-accurate uniforms of all sorts, this wonderful volume offers chapters on various topics of interest for those of us who were easily captivated by the dawn this depiction of modern Britain.  The all-inclusive Table of Contents lists: FAMILY LIFE, SOCIETY, CHANGE, LIFE IN SERVICE, STYLE, HOUSE AND ESTATE, ROMANCE, WAR, and BEHIND THE SCENES.An alphabetic cast list is provided on page 298.<br /><br />Reading this book is somewhat of a challenge for the text (even a sentence) is interrupted by pages of photos both from the production and of posters and relevant material.  Members of the cast and crew were interviewed to provide interesting quotes re their take on their roles and costumes. The author also includes delightful passages from the actual script which serve to highlight the topics of each chapter.<br /><br />So why do DOWNTON ABBEY aficionados devour this volume? Why to relive the sumptuous upstairs/downstairs soap opera on a grand scale.  (Upstairs: Desperate for an Heir, Downstairs: Eager for Advancement.) As the distinctions of Place (\\"mind your betters!\\" as butler Hudson would growl) gradually during and after WW1 both extremes of society struggled to adapt--some more than others. Although a delightful companion to the series, this excellent book was as meticulously researched as were the sets, costumes and props; it can proudly stand as a Sociology textbook--an accurate source of previously hidden details about this era British 20th Century Britain. Your favorite characters beyond and behind the \\"green baize door\\" remain eternally young--helping a new generation of readers and viewers recapture the spirit of Edwardian England.Eager for Advancement.) B006QS1XRO|4.0|Richard C. Morais' debut novel, The Hundred Foot Journey, is a travel book for anyone who has ever watched The Food Network and thought, \\"Wherever that kitchen is, that's where I want to go.\\"<br /><br />Food is the language of this book. The character of Hassan Haji sometimes struggles with issues of identity and belonging as he travels from Mumbai to London to Lumiere to Paris, but always this struggle is phrased in terms of food: to make curry or frogs legs, to seek out tiffin boxes or fish and chips. Even his Muslim identity is mentioned rarely except when relating to diet: to eat pork or not. Ultimately Hassan's true identity is food. His religion is food. His ethnicity is food. His blood runs with curry and wine and butter and garlic and the jus of fresh oysters.<br /><br />It's as though Pi Patel from Life of Pi was experiencing some sort of cosmic opposites day: an Indian boy, instead of trying to find his way home while adrift and alone, is continually travelling further afield while being wrapped in the memories and support of his family; where Pi invented stories to quell his loneliness, Hassan sometimes longs for solitude so he may study the stories of the ancient cookbooks which surround him; where Pi's starvation was his constant companion, Hassan's one constant is food.<br /><br />To read more about this book or to read my other reviews, please visit my blog: [...]

And I see that approach to any god, as a significantly flawed approach to any god one might choose to believe in.<br /><br />I find science and the facts that the scientific method can elicit to be far more than \\"dry, yeastless factuality.\\" Martel wants us to look for \\"the better story\\" outside of the facts, or scientific reality. I reject that intellectually weak and insincere premise. It is that kind of thinking that leads Creationist to reject scientific fact simply because they don't like the story.<br /><br />Am I glad I read it? Yes, and I would recommend it for anyone. Yes, but yes for reasons very different than Martel might have expected. His allegory is a flawed one, and his allegory brought more clearly into focus why I have such disdain for organized religion, and especially the lies that Creationists believe because they want \\"the better story.\\"<br /><br />The truly better story is always the truth elicited by the scientific method, especially for those with the intellectual curiosity and intellectual rigor to uncover the truth of that story. Though I have no problem if that scientific story is told through metaphor or allegory. That is something I would in fact recommend.

1400052173|4.0|When I first sat down to read this book, I expected it to be somewhat dry. I was wrong. It has a rather literary novel feel to it, and had it not been from the disclosure that this was based on the true story of Henrietta Lacks (and her family), it would be easy to mistake it for (science) fiction.<br /><br />The book touches on issues of ethics, especially in the field of medicine, and how the HeLa cells are still being used today. I was amazed at how poignant the story was, despite its scientific nature. Rebecca Skloot managed to deliver a balanced story delving into the life of Henrietta Lacks and her (unwitting) contribution to science and its effects on her family and the world of medicine. And it's not sugarcoated, though it is quite respectful. It would have been all too easy to exploit Deborah's poverty-stricken situation, but Skloot kept the human-factor in check.<br /><br />A number of times I was brought to tears while reading and while the book doesn't offer any answers to questions posed, it does make one stop and consider what might have been taken from them in the name of scientific advancement and it also makes one think about what is held most dear.
